    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer13015205164181134246">Stark, brooding, and enormously controversial when first published in 1905, this astonishing novel juxtaposes impressions of fin-de-siècle Stockholm against the psychological landscape of a man besieged by obsession. Lonely and introspective, Doctor Glas has long felt an instinctive hostility toward the odious local minister. So when the minister’s beautiful wife complains</span>
  <span id="freeText13015205164181134246" style="display:none">Stark, brooding, and enormously controversial when first published in 1905, this astonishing novel juxtaposes impressions of fin-de-siècle Stockholm against the psychological landscape of a man besieged by obsession. Lonely and introspective, Doctor Glas has long felt an instinctive hostility toward the odious local minister. So when the minister’s beautiful wife complains of her husband’s oppressive sexual attentions, Doctor Glas finds himself contemplating murder. A masterpiece of enduring power, <b>Doctor Glas</b><i> </i>confronts a chilling moral quandary with gripping intensity.</span>

<a href="/questions/314881-i-love-the-poem-that-is-in-it-but-i-am">I love the poem that is in it, but i am Dutch and i want to read this book in Dutch?

 “People want to be loved; 
failing that admired; 
failing that feared; 
failing that hated and despised. 

They want to evoke some sort of sentiment. The soul shudders before oblivion and seeks connection at any price.” 

― Hjalmar Söderberg, Doctor Glas</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2398422020?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literature">literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2398422020" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4586276563135219376"><i>
  <b>I wonder what it feels like to have a crime on one's conscience.</b>
</i><br/><br/>In less than 200 pages, this secret marvel of literature rivets, subverts, perturbs. <br/><br/>Originally published in Sweden in 1905, Doctor Glas is another frightening character unsuited to his profession - a man who has no business being anyone's doctor (see his buddies Lecter, Frankenstein, and Moreau over there in the corner). He finds bodies repulsive and regards most people with distainful disenchantment. Not surprising then, that </span>
  <span id="freeText4586276563135219376" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>I wonder what it feels like to have a crime on one's conscience.</b>
</i><br /><br />In less than 200 pages, this secret marvel of literature rivets, subverts, perturbs. <br /><br />Originally published in Sweden in 1905, Doctor Glas is another frightening character unsuited to his profession - a man who has no business being anyone's doctor (see his buddies Lecter, Frankenstein, and Moreau over there in the corner). He finds bodies repulsive and regards most people with distainful disenchantment. Not surprising then, that he's isolated and introspective. He's also sexually stunted. At thirty something, Glas has never been with a woman, and judges intercourse to be undignified at best. Knowing all this makes reading his private diary highly interesting, turning the reader into a shameless rubber-necker.<br /><br />When the wife of a despicable old clergyman comes to his office in need of help, Glas has the opportunity to act, to do something of importance. He's trapped in a quandary not unlike Raskolnikov's: rid the world of a thoroughly awful person, or not? If yes, why? <br /><br /><i>
  <b>What was it that I had been dreaming? Always and the same thing. That I killed the clergyman.</b>
</i><br /><br />As much as it is about "the act", it's even more about the "why". So, while you're pulled along by tension created by the voyeuristic experience of reading Glas' diary (will he or won't he?), you're also engaged in a philosophical argument good enough for readers of Dostoevsky - but in far fewer pages.<br /><br />Despite that it's over a century old, <i>Doctor Glas</i> has a distinctively modern appeal. I was pulled into the stream-of-consciousness narrative from the first page. It's frank, dramatic, and controversial. Söderberg takes a close look at human nature, the desire to connect. He also makes a fascinating comparison between the pursuit of enduring happiness vs. the immediacy of pleasure. <br /><br />What I wonder is why this book isn't gobbled up more, like the delicious, vicious delight that it is.<br /><br />Brooding, dark, intellectual. Gimme gimme gimme.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4586276563135219376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2965645530888901869" style="display:none">In this short strong novel we get debates about light pollution (you can’t see the stars any more), tourism (there are just too many), assisted dying (it should be legal), abortion (likewise) and, because of the fear of germs, we also get an interesting discussion about whether communion wine should be administered in church by capsules rather than unhygienic shared chalices.<br /><br />And then the main arc of the plot is all about marital rape which became illegal in the UK in 1991.<br /> So I kept checking if this book was written in 1990, or 1980, or 1970 but no, the copyright page kept saying 1905. <div id="review_398008455" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/398008455" />
    <a title="Ema" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5746897-ema"><img alt="Ema" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394635581p2/5746897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/398008455?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ema" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ema" href="/user/show/5746897-ema">Ema</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=favs">favs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=bookish-gems">bookish-gems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=sweden">sweden</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer398008455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer180712704478732891">This short novel is a melancholic guide of Stockholm. It is a compendium of thoughts and reflections upon life from a peculiar character. It is a story of alienation and unrequited love. And it is also the story of a planned murder. <br/><br/>Although more than a hundred years old, this novel feels as fresh as if written yesterday. The only hint of its age are depictions of places in Stockholm that no longer exist and some obsolete debates (on abortion, for example), but otherwise the language doesn't fel</span>
  <span id="freeText180712704478732891" style="display:none">This short novel is a melancholic guide of Stockholm. It is a compendium of thoughts and reflections upon life from a peculiar character. It is a story of alienation and unrequited love. And it is also the story of a planned murder. <br /><br />Although more than a hundred years old, this novel feels as fresh as if written yesterday. The only hint of its age are depictions of places in Stockholm that no longer exist and some obsolete debates (on abortion, for example), but otherwise the language doesn't fell dated and the ideas are as universal as ever.<br /><br />Tyko Gabriel Glas is a young doctor in Stockholm. He is intelligent and well-off, but his cynical view of life has made him a solitary man. He has a habit of falling for women that are already in love with other men, as only they seem to spark his interest. But he is painfully aware that no woman can truly get to know and accept him.<br /><br />He spends his evenings alone, strolling through the city streets and writing in his diary. His nature is that of a spectator, he prefers to observe people from afar, rather than spend time in their company. On the other hand, he doesn't have a high opinion of himself, either.<br /><br /><i>I'm going through grey days and dark moments. I'm not happy. But I wouldn't take the place of anybody else; my heart cringes at the thought that I could be one of my acquaintances.[...] I don't particularly like myself, neither the shell, nor the core. But I wouldn't want to be anybody else.</i><br /><br />Pastor Gregorius is the man he despises and dreads the most and, after the preacher's beautiful wife asks for his help in a delicate matter, Doctor Glas abandons his reserved position and decides to interfere. But how far is he willing to go for the woman who gradually becomes the object of his affection, he who used to hide behind the screen of duty when it came to breaking the law?<br /><br />I can see why this novel was considered controversial in 1905 - Hjalmar Söderberg touches some sensitive topics: abortion, assisted suicide (which he envisions as a human right in the future), sexuality, morality and religion. In my opinion, the novel hasn't lost its flavor, so it's totally worth reading it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="180712704478732891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_479047629" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/479047629" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/479047629?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789100581527" href="/book/show/10780909-doktor-glas">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who is interested in the human condition or has seen Stockholm</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=swedish-norwegian-and-danish">swedish-norwegian-and-danish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts">too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer479047629&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer479047629" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5312058207078430023">This book has become quite well-known recently due to the theatre production starring Krister Henriksson. We saw it in London a few months ago, and it is indeed very good; but I was surprised to discover that the book is substantially different. <br/><br/>The eponymous Glas, as most people now know, is a doctor in late 19th century Stockholm who kills the husband of one of his patients. Fru Gregorius is young and beautiful; her husband is a hypocritical old priest whose very presence makes Glas feel physi</span>
  <span id="freeText5312058207078430023" style="display:none">This book has become quite well-known recently due to the theatre production starring Krister Henriksson. We saw it in London a few months ago, and it is indeed very good; but I was surprised to discover that the book is substantially different. <br><br>The eponymous Glas, as most people now know, is a doctor in late 19th century Stockholm who kills the husband of one of his patients. Fru Gregorius is young and beautiful; her husband is a hypocritical old priest whose very presence makes Glas feel physically sick. Gregorius's wife is secretly unfaithful to him, and Glas is the only person she has told. She cannot stand the fact that her husband still insists on having sex with her, and asks Glas if he can do anything. <br><br>In the play, Glas's motivation seems clear. A lonely person with no one to love, he falls for the beautiful wife's charm and agrees to everything she asks for; he came across as rather like M. Hire in Patrice Leconte's film, though things work out better for him.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1381267219i/4618445.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="Hire" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>But I experienced the book in another way. The central puzzle is why Glas murders the priest. The clear lines of the play are blurred; he is indeed very taken with the wife, but he could equally well have responded to Fröken Mertens, who is also young and beautiful, and clearly loves him. He chooses to ignore her, and there are more factors in play. Glas has always found sex disgusting. The girl he loved as a young man died in a tragic accident shortly after he kissed her for the first time. And so many of his female patients have begged him for abortions. He has consistently turned them down, but he's wondered more and more often if he's been right to do that. <br><br>Somehow, sex and death have got mixed up in Glas's cold, analytical mind. The book is the diary he starts keeping as he tries to disentangle things. He scrupulously weighs up the pros and cons of his various choices, discusses the morality of the situation, plans carefully to minimize the chance of being discovered, considers whether he's likely to suffer pangs of remorse. But after he's killed Gregorius, he still doesn't really know why he's done it. He rereads his notes, and decides he probably did the right thing, but he's not sure. All he can see is an infinite web of cause and effect stretching out into the past. He wishes he hadn't investigated it so carefully. <br><br>The book, too, has its own web of causes and effects. Several of the characters come from earlier Söderberg novels. The hunchbacked watch-maker who sells Glas the case in which he keeps the cyanide pills also appears in <i>Förvillelser</i>, Söderberg's first book; there, he marries the shop-girl that Thomas Weber gets pregnant. Martin Birck, a character that Glas often meets, is the hero of <i>Martin Bircks Ungdom</i>, written four years earlier, where he is pretty clearly Söderberg himself. <br><br>And although people at the time hated <i>Doktor Glas</i> and called it implausible and immoral, it has aged well. The edition I read has a postscript briefly explaining how it was chosen in 2002 to be the first book in the series <i>Stockholm läser</i> - "Stockholm reads". I think Söderberg would have been touched by this gesture of appreciation from the city he loved so much, and I am sure Swedes, and in particular people from Stockholm, will carry on reading it for a long time to come.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1381317223i/4697432._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="290" alt="Stockholm" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="5312058207078430023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review479047629">

There are some passages that I came back to read again and again. <br/><br/>Its a strange, insightful and interesting book written at the beginning of 20th century. The story is about a young doctor who either pities or despises his patients and plays the judge and the jury and the executioner sometimes.<br/><br/>The book makes you question fundamental issues such as morals, ethics and faith.</span>

<div id="review_708476462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/708476462" />
    <a title="Issicratea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23268828-issicratea"><img alt="Issicratea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378040602p2/23268828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/708476462?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Issicratea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Issicratea" href="/user/show/23268828-issicratea">Issicratea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23268828-issicratea?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23268828-issicratea?shelf=1900-1950">1900-1950</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer708476462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13666284540530439824">I rather fell in love with this book. It’s concise—more a novella than a novel—and the plot is extremely simple, hardly more than a vehicle for the first person narrator’s dark philosophical meditations. But that doesn’t prevent it from being a compelling read. <br/><br/>The novel’s great strength is its voice. It takes the form of a series of diary entries over a few summer months in Stockholm, in an unidentified year close to the time of composition (it was published in 1905, apparently much to the épat</span>
  <span id="freeText13666284540530439824" style="display:none">I rather fell in love with this book. It’s concise—more a novella than a novel—and the plot is extremely simple, hardly more than a vehicle for the first person narrator’s dark philosophical meditations. But that doesn’t prevent it from being a compelling read. <br /><br />The novel’s great strength is its voice. It takes the form of a series of diary entries over a few summer months in Stockholm, in an unidentified year close to the time of composition (it was published in 1905, apparently much to the épatement of the bourgeois, touching as it does on scandalous subjects such as abortion). The diarist is a thirty-something doctor, Tyko Gabriel Glas, whose smooth social surface conceals a turbid inner life, compounded of thwarted romanticism, sexual phobia, and a proto-existentialist consciousness of the meaningless of the universe. Glas is unhappy and self-hating, yet at the same time half in love with his outsiderdom, as his astute and cynical alter-ego journalist friend Markel remarks at one point.<br /><br /><i>There are people who lack all talent for happiness and know it with painful, inescapable clarity. Such people don’t strive for happiness, but to bring a little order and distinction to their unhappiness … Glas is one of them.</i><br /><br />Glas’s attempt to bring order to his unhappiness takes a peculiar form, arising from his half-chivalric, half-erotic obsession with a pastor’s young wife, and his corresponding feeling of repulsion for her much older husband, who clearly embodies for him much of what he loathes in his culture. The meetings with husband and wife that punctuate the novel are beautifully handled, especially those with the wife, whose attitude to the doctor is intriguingly ambiguous. (Does she feel the genuine admiration and meeting of souls she suggests? Or has she intuited his infatuation and decided to exploit it?) I shan’t go into the way in which this situation is resolved—or not resolved—as it would be difficult to do so without spoilers. Suffice to say I thought the ending superb.<br /><br /><i>Dr Glas</i> is one of those books with nominally dark subject-matter that is far from depressing to read. It’s a delight, in fact. The protagonist’s diaristic meanderings are fresh and eccentric and unpredictable, and the writing is wonderful (in so far as one can tell in translation). I found the Stockholm setting evocative as well.<br /><br />As a sample, here is Dr Glas, at a dramatic moment in the novel, distracted by his consciousness streaming off in an unexpected direction:<br /><br /><i>Suppose I allow her to love me? I’m so lonely. Last winter I had a tabby cat, but he ran off come spring. I’m put in mind of him now, as the light of the first autumn hearth fire dances over the flame-red rug. He’d lie there, just in front of the hearth and purr. In vain I exerted myself to win his affection. He lapped up my milk and warmed himself by my fire, but his heart remained cold. What became of you, Smutter? You had decadent tendencies. I fear you may have fallen into dissolute ways, if you are still roaming the earth. Last night I heard a cat’s cry in the churchyard and I was sure I recognized your voice.</i><br /><br />I love those rhythms. There’s an edition of this novel by Harvill Press with what sounds like an excellent introduction by Margaret Atwood, to judge from this sample:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/books/2002/oct/26/classics.margaretatwood">http://www.guardian.co.uk/books/2002/...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13666284540530439824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_699344459" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/699344459" />
    <a title="Lynda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13400128-lynda"><img alt="Lynda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558931714p2/13400128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/699344459?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynda" href="/user/show/13400128-lynda">Lynda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18360538-doctor-glas">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=ebooks-amazon">ebooks-amazon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=z-read-2013">z-read-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=author-nordic">author-nordic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer699344459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17678089908719910069"><b>"I'm sitting at my open window now and writing this - for whom? Not for any friend or girlfriend, scarcely even for myself, for I don't read today what I wrote yesterday, and I won't be reading this tomorrow [...]<br/>What I'm writing on these pages isn't a confession; to whom should I confess? I don't relate everything about myself. I relate only what it pleases me to relate, but I never say anything untrue. Not that I can lie away my souls misery, if miserable it must be."</b><br/><br/><b>Synopsis</b><br/>Doctor Glas, an ep</span>
  <span id="freeText17678089908719910069" style="display:none"><b>"I'm sitting at my open window now and writing this - for whom? Not for any friend or girlfriend, scarcely even for myself, for I don't read today what I wrote yesterday, and I won't be reading this tomorrow [...]<br>What I'm writing on these pages isn't a confession; to whom should I confess? I don't relate everything about myself. I relate only what it pleases me to relate, but I never say anything untrue. Not that I can lie away my souls misery, if miserable it must be."</b><br><br><b>Synopsis</b><br>Doctor Glas, an epistolary novel by Hjalmar Söderberg, tells the story of a physician in 19th-century Sweden who deals with moral and love issues. Published in 1905, it takes the form of a journal over the course of a sultry summer. Doctor Glas is the main character, while the antagonist is Reverend Gregorius, a morally corrupt clergyman. Gregorius' beautiful young wife confides in Doctor Glas that her sex life is making her miserable and asks for his help. Glas, in love with her, agrees to help even though she already has another adulterous lover. He attempts to intervene, but the Reverend refuses to give up his "marital rights" - she must have sex* with him whether she likes it or not. So, in order to make his love happy, he begins to plot her husband's murder. <br><br><i>*A husband's right to sexual intercourse was assured by law in several ways. It was impossible for a husband to be tried for rape, because by marrying the wife had 'given herself up' sexually to her husband; cohabiting with her husband meant he could rape her with impunity. A woman's pleasure was supposed to be derived purely and entirely from making him happy and her only sex-related desire was for motherhood. Any women who went outside of this rigid boundary (e.g. those who admitted to sexual desires, had sex with men outside of marriage, unmarried mothers, lesbians and prostitutes) were labelled 'immoral', 'impure', 'scandalous', 'fallen' or 'mentally ill'. A woman who left her husband or was divorced would find herself outcast from respectable society, although she did nothing wrong.</i> <br><br><b>Author</b><br>Hjalmar Söderberg (1869-1941) was one of the most distinguished of Scandinavian novelists. He was born and raised in Stockholm and spent the last twenty-five years of his life in Copenhagen. After working as a civil servant, he turned to journalism and eventually devoted himself full-time to a literary career. In addition to novels he wrote short stories and plays as well as literary criticism and philosophical works about religion. He has been praised for his fictional vignettes of Stockholm life and for being a forerunner in the use of psychoanalytic theory and stream-of-consciousness in his fiction. Söderberg's novels include Confusions, Martin Birck's Youth, and The Serious Game; Doctor Glas is regarded as his masterpiece.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1378488380p8/841512.jpg" width="200" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>My review</b><br>Stark, brooding, and enormously controversial when first published in 1905, this astonishing novel juxtaposes impressions of fin-de-siècle Stockholm against the psychological landscape of a man besieged by obsession. Lonely and introspective, solitary and melancholy, Dr Glas is a sad, tragic figure and a tortured soul. <br><br>Loneliness, in one form or another, provides the leitmotif of this book. <br><br><i>"It makes me feel as if there's no one in the world lonely at this moment but I. I, doctor of medicine Tyko Gabriel Glas, who sometimes helps others but has never been able to help himself, and who, on entering his thirty-fourth year of life, has never yet been with a woman."<br><br>"Nothing diminishes a man and drags him down so much as the consciousness of not being loved."<br><br>"People want to be loved; failing that admired; failing that feared; failing that hated and despised. They want to evoke some sort of sentiment. The soul shudders before oblivion and seeks connection at any price."</i><br><br>I found the emotional pull of Dr. Glas's loneliness to be very strong, especially during his grey days and black hours. I felt so bad for him I just wanted to take him in my arms. Equally there were times when my repulsion of him were as tenacious as those he held for Reverend Gregorius. <br><br>Doctor Glas is one of those marvellous books that appears as fresh and vivid now as on the day it was published. And even in translation, the integrity of the writing compels. It deals with issues such as abortion, women's rights, suicide, euthanasia, and eugenics. This is a powerful novel. <br><br>As the English writer William Sansom has said, "In most of its writing and much of the frankness of its thought, it might have been written tomorrow."<br><br>An impressive book and certainly recommended. <br><br><u>Postscript:</u> In 2012 a restaurant called Doktor Glas was opened in Gothenburg in recognition of the book's place in Swedish literature.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1378487964p8/841503.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I am dedicating this review to another Goodreads reader <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/3920693-richard" rel="nofollow noopener">Richard</a> because he very kindly and patiently assisted a fellow reader and non-techhead in learning the tricks of adding a photo. Thanks Richard!<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="17678089908719910069" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1399707" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1875218098838502945">It's a shame this novel isn't more readily available in the US. I read it for a Swedish literature course while I studied in Stockholm and fell in love with it before the end of the first page.<br/><br/>Usually diary novels come across as trite and forced, at least to me. While the whole concept of diary novels is rather dubious at best (can you recall pages of dialog you've had with people off the top of your head?), some do it better than others. Söderberg handles it masterfully; you'll find yourself ha</span>
  <span id="freeText1875218098838502945" style="display:none">It's a shame this novel isn't more readily available in the US. I read it for a Swedish literature course while I studied in Stockholm and fell in love with it before the end of the first page.<br /><br />Usually diary novels come across as trite and forced, at least to me. While the whole concept of diary novels is rather dubious at best (can you recall pages of dialog you've had with people off the top of your head?), some do it better than others. Söderberg handles it masterfully; you'll find yourself hard-pressed to put the book down. <br /><br />A short read, to be sure, but by no means unintelligent. Söderberg packs in a lot of food for thought on nearly every topic under the sun: euthanasia, religion, adultery, science...the list goes on. <br /><br />Absolutely flawless. If you happen to come across it, please, pick it up! You won't regret it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1875218098838502945" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2245597865" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2245597865" />
    <a title="Edita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5685453-edita"><img alt="Edita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595755309p2/5685453.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2245597865?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edita" href="/user/show/5685453-edita">Edita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5685453-edita?shelf=hjalmar-s%C3%B6derberg">hjalmar-söderberg</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2245597865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9980445416686310997">There have to be some people with whom one can be sincere . . .<br/> *<br/>It’s strange to sit here trying to recapture a mood, a way of thinking, from long ago. This sort of thing makes one feel the passage of time. The law of change, as the preacher said (for that matter he’d stolen it from some Ibsen play). It’s like examining an old photograph of yourself. And I also thought: how much longer can I still have left to meander without purpose through this world of riddles and dreams and inexplicable phen</span>
  <span id="freeText9980445416686310997" style="display:none">There have to be some people with whom one can be sincere . . .<br /> *<br />It’s strange to sit here trying to recapture a mood, a way of thinking, from long ago. This sort of thing makes one feel the passage of time. The law of change, as the preacher said (for that matter he’d stolen it from some Ibsen play). It’s like examining an old photograph of yourself. And I also thought: how much longer can I still have left to meander without purpose through this world of riddles and dreams and inexplicable phenomena?<br /> *<br />I felt that I would never again will such a thing or commit such an act. Was it all an illusion? But still,I’d acted according to my best judgment. I’d weighed and tested, for and against. I’d gotten to the bottom of the matter. Was that an illusion? It didn’t matter. The music now came to the mysterious leitmotif: “Thou shalt not ask!” And in this mystical series of notes and these four words it seemed to me I could find the sudden revelation of an ancient and hidden wisdom. “Thou shalt not ask!” Don’t get to the bottom of things: if you do, you yourself will founder. Don’t seek the truth: you won’t find it and will lose yourself. “Thou shalt not ask!” The amount of truth that is beneficial will come to you with no effort; it’s mixed with delusions and lies, but that’s for the sake of your health—in pure form it would burn your insides. Don’t try to cleanse your soul of lies—many other things will be lost, too, things you haven’t considered. You’ll lose your bearings and everything that’s dear to you. “Thou shalt not ask!<br />*<br />And I’m finally beginning to suspect that perhaps we’re not meant to understand life. All these furious attempts to explain and understand, this ongoing quest for the truth, may be a dead end. We bless the sun because we live the exact distance from it that is beneficial. A few million miles closer or farther away and we would burn or freeze to death. What if the truth is like the sun?<br /> *<br />. . . I’ve become more and more impossible socially. I forget to respond when people talk to me. Often I don’t hear what they say. I wonder if I’m starting to go deaf?<br /> And all these masks! All of them wear masks. And what’s more, it’s their greatest virtue. I wouldn’t want to see them unmasked. I wouldn’t want to appear unmasked myself, either. Not to them!<br /> But to whom?</span>
  <a data-text-id="9980445416686310997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer275293640" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10474425138459443613">For my swedish review, read further down. <br/><br/>This is a difficult book to review. I haven't had time to read a lot this week, but I think that has been in my favour because I've had time to consider and reflect about the content, and have realized what a philosophical author Söderberg was.<br/><br/>We get to follow the main character through his perception of the world, in the form of diary notes. He finds comfort in writing (so much that he actually doesn't get rid of them later on, even though he suspects t</span>
  <span id="freeText10474425138459443613" style="display:none">For my swedish review, read further down. <br /><br />This is a difficult book to review. I haven't had time to read a lot this week, but I think that has been in my favour because I've had time to consider and reflect about the content, and have realized what a philosophical author Söderberg was.<br /><br />We get to follow the main character through his perception of the world, in the form of diary notes. He finds comfort in writing (so much that he actually doesn't get rid of them later on, even though he suspects them to be a big danger to him). Soon enough, he sees a problem which has to be solved. A woman is in distress, and he is the man to help her. The repercussions for interfering, though, haven't crossed his mind.<br /><br />Dr Glas reminds me of Dostoyesky's Raskolnikov in many ways, if a more rational, cynical and remorseless version. Glas is a viewer, who doesn't want to participate or attend his surroundings. He lives through others' destinies and thereby tries to interpret the world. He struggles with a sense of melancholy and meaninglessness, while he's, simultaneously, obsessed with attraction, love and sexuality. Since he views the world through the eyes of others, he also imagines their view of him. The conviction that he is considered a disappointing and worthless individual naturally gives him that opinion of himself.<br /><br />His constant sense of alienation makes him cynical. He doesn't accept darwinism, since that plunges him deeper into his feeling of meaninglessness. I think that is because he desperately wants to be a part of a bigger plan, and to be able to feel he is, he ascribes a purpose to the people in his life. The world isn't his, so he builds one which is - a theater with characters he think he knows and expect things from - a world where he is important and might perform great things. Unfortunately, this has a price. It comes to a point where he has to confront himself. When the gap between your view of the world and others gets too big, the illusion becomes visible, and thereby unsustainable. Inevitably, you must question it, whereby the illusion is shattered - which can be associated with the name Glas (glass). He is fragile because of his dependence of his own world structure. I don't think Glas is transparent, exactly - as his name implies - but empty (and thereby unimportant), because of his lack of self-worth. When he is forgotten in a dangerous place, he is easily crushed by mistake.<br /><br /><br />Spoilers!<br /><br />This isn't a story of a man driven by poverty or injustice. As a doctor, with his own practice, he eats at fine restaurants and have a nice home. This man is driven by something else. Söderberg stated that you want to be loved, otherwise admired, otherwise feared, otherwise detested and despised. Glas' final decision to act makes him something. But what started with a small idea, slowly destroys his own life, that is very prominent. It can be described as a darkness that expands and eventually consumes him - not in form of a bad conscience, but in shattering his construction of the world and himself. He has crossed the line. The big purpose he ascribed to himself was to free the woman of her torments. At first he felt he had done something, he felt alive. He had made a difference. He had influenced the world, and thereby he must be a part of it. That didn't last long. He soon felt that a change had come over him; that he was changed, that the world was changed. He plunges into hopelessness when he'll never be able to win her heart. The act which freed her of her prison, imprisoned him.<br /><br />Söderberg investigated many, at the time, controversial topics. <br />* Sexuality, abortion, rape, obligations, moral, religion and euthanasia are discussed. The book was very provoking back in the early 20th century. It's fascinating that an author, hundred years ago, was considering abortion and euthanasia as a human right, and dared to challenge religion and make the very priest, Gregorius, seem like a hypocrite. Perhaps Glas isn't always right, but to question was very important in a conservative time.<br /><br />* Glas' view of obligations was a general theme. He is controlled by obligations; as a doctor, as a fellow human being and as a man. But what is obligation, really? Can it be the opposite of moral? The obligations as a doctor prevent him from fulfilling his patients dreams - he couldn't do something as unethical as lie and murder, to free them from their torments, could he? But his obligations as a fellow human being let him do that, even if illegal. They are called compassion and commitment. The discussion that takes place inside Glas' head when his beliefs and convictions argue is extremely fascinating.<br /><br />This is a rare swedish book, without a preachy voice. The big questions the book arouses are<br />* Is it right to kill a human being to save another?<br />* How could anyone be entirely sure that it is a selfless act that will make the world a better place?<br />* Is it possible to live in a self-constructed world? If not, what happens when the illusion is crushed?<br /><br />Certain things are revealed, but in the end, you have to interpret the story and decide whether Dr Glas deed made a difference or not.<br /><br /><br />Recension<br /><br />Det här är en otroligt svår bok att recensera. Jag har inte haft möjlighet att läsa så mycket åt gången de senaste dagarna, men det har varit en fördel eftersom det fått mig att hinna reflektera över bokens alla intressanta och skarpsynta kommentarer och jag har insett vilken filosofisk författare Söderberg var.<br /><br />När Dr Glas misstänker att någonting omoraliskt och rent av brottsligt pågår utser han sig själv till den som ska lösa problemet. Bokens struktur består av dagboksanteckningar. Glas finner ett umgänge i sin dagbok och den är så viktig för honom att han senare behåller den, trots att den utgör en fara för honom. Bokens huvudkaraktär Dr Glas är som en mer rationell version av Dostoyevskys Raskolnikov, men cynisk och utan samvetskval. Glas är en betraktare, som inte gärna vill delta i sin omgivning. Han lever genom andras livsöden, och därför blir det viktigt att genom dem tolka en så intressant bild som möjligt av världen. Han brottas med melankoli och känslan av meningslöshet, men är samtidigt besatt av attraktion, kärlek och sexualitet. Eftersom han betraktar världen genom andras ögon, föreställer han sig också hur han själv betraktas. Tanken att han betraktas som misslyckad och värdelös ger honom naturligtvis den uppfattningen om sig själv. <br /><br />Dr Glas konstanta känsla av utanförskap ligger till grund för hans cyniska inställning till livet och känslan av meningslöshet. Han tycker inte om Darwinism, och orsaken till det tror jag beror på viljan att vara en del av en större plan, en djupare mening. Världen är inte hans. Därför bygger han upp en värld och för att skapa mening tillskriver han hela tiden personer i sin omgivning ett syfte. De blir karaktärer i hans teater som han tror sig känna och har förväntningar på. I den världen får han ett viktigt uppdrag och kan uträtta stordåd. Dessvärre leder detta till att han förr eller senare tvingas möta lögnen. När gapet mellan världen och hans uppbyggnad av den är för stort, (eller kanske det är gapet mellan hans egen tolkning och andras) blir illusionen synlig och med ens ohållbar. Han tvingas då ifrågasätta den, varpå illusionen krossas. Man kan göra tydliga kopplingar till namnet Glas, för han är bräcklig i det avseendet att han är beroende av sin egen världsuppbyggnad. Glas är inte transparent som sitt namn antyder, men har uppfattningen att inte bli sedd och blir därmed ett tomt glas som lätt glöms bort, och befinner han sig då på en farlig plats kan han krossas av misstag.<br /><br /><br />Spoilers!<br /><br />Det här är inte en berättelse om en man, driven av fattigdom eller orättvisa. Som läkare har han råd med ett bekvämt vardagsliv; han äter på attraktiva restauranger och har ett någorlunda fint hem. Han drivs av något annat. <br />"Man vill bli älskad, i brist därpå beundrad, i brist därpå fruktad, i brist därpå avskydd och föraktad. Man vill ingiva människorna någon slags känsla. Själen ryser för tomrummet...".<br />Glas beslut att agera gjorde honom till någonting, men förgör honom sakta, som ett mörker som sakta expanderar och förtär honom slutligen, inte i form av dåligt samvete, utan genom att krossa hans världsbild. Det syfte han tillskrivit sig, att rädda kvinnan, ger honom en egendomlig känsla av liv. Han har inflytande över världen, alltså måste han vara en del av den. Men snart övergår känslan i en mörk, negativ hopplöshet, då han vet att han aldrig kommer vinna hennes hjärta. Han känner att världen är förändrad, att han är förändrad. När han befriade kvinnan, gav han upp sin egen frihet, och det osynliga fängelset kan han aldrig lämna.<br /><br />Söderberg behandlade många ämnen.<br />* Under den tiden kontroversiella ämnen som abort, våldtäkt, sexualitet, religion och dödshjälp diskuteras och provocerar, inte minst under sin tid, men visar samtidigt på en insikt hos författaren, en modern vindpust i det tidiga 1900-talets vackra men konservativa Stockholm. <br />"Den dag skall och måste komma, då rätten att dö blir erkänd som en långt viktigare och mera oförytterlig människorätt än den att lägga en sedel i valurnan. Och när den tiden är mogen, skall varje obotligt sjuk - och varje "brottsling" också - äga rätt till läkarens hjälp, om han vill befrielsen."<br /><br />* Moral är någonting man inte kan följa till punkt och pricka om man är en god människa, utan bör mer ses som seden i ett specifikt samhälle, som bör respekteras men inte nödvändigtvis ses som det enda rätta. <br />"Jag vet och erkänner, att den gängse moralen så väl som den borgerliga lagen i sina stora allmänna grunddrag uttrycker en rättsuppfattning, som är frukten av oöverskådliga tiders från släkte till släkte ärvda, långsamt ökade och ändrade erfarenhet om de nödvändigaste villkoren för människors samlevnad med varandra. Jag vet, att i stort sett dessa lagar någorlunda allmänt måste respekteras, om livet alls skall kunna levas här på jorden av varelser som vi, varelser, otänkbara inom varje annan ram än samhällsorganisationens och uppfödda med alla dess växlande rätter, bibliotek och museer, polis och vattenledning, ljus på gatorna, natthämtning, vaktparad, predikningar, operabalett och så vidare. men jag vet också, att de människor som det har varit något bevänt med aldrig ha tagit dessa lagar pedantiskt. Moralen hör till husgerådet, inte till gudarna. Den skall begagnas; den skall inte härska."<br /><br />* Plikten är viktig och ett genomgående tema. Men vad är egentligen plikt? Glas styrs av plikten han har som läkare, men som skiljer sig från plikten han har mot sina medmänniskor. Kan plikten vara omoralisk? Plikten som läkare motsätter sig både att uppfylla patienters dröm om att få lämna livet och att fria dem från våldtäkt. För inte kan han väl göra något så drastiskt som att ljuga och mörda för att befria patienter från deras plågor? Samtidigt har han en plikt som medmänniska. En plikt som heter medlidande och åtagande. Den diskussion som pågår inne i Glas huvud när hans övertygelser argumenterar och krockar med varandra är oerhört fascinerande.<br /><br />* Ofta beskrivs flera perspektiv, en minnesvärd del är den om månens alla ansikten, och på samma sätt kan världen i stort betraktas. Det känns som att Glas inte riktigt vet vilket perspektiv han själv ska utgå ifrån när han reflekterar över den drastiska handling han funderar på att genomföra. Det här är den största likheten med Dostoyevskys Raskolnikov. Men Dr Glas är en lättare version, utan den där tunga, komplicerade känslan av samvetskval eller gråzoner som leder in Raskolnikov i den febriga dimman.<br /><br />Söderberg vågade inte bara provocera med kontroversiella ämnen, han var också intresserad av att förolämpa det svenska språket, som många nog kan instämma saknar halvmesyrer.<br />"Det är på det hela taget ett förbannat språk vi ha. Orden trampa varandra på tårna och knuffa varandra i rännstenen. Och allt blir så påtagligt och rått. Inga halvtoner, inga lätta antydningar och mjuka övergångar. Ett språk som tycks vara skapat till bruk för den outrotliga påbelvanan att plumpa ut med sanningen i alla väder."<br /><br />Det här är en ytterst intressant svensk roman som är väldigt ovanlig. Den lämnar mycket åt läsaren och avstår från predikan. De frågor som väcks av Dr Glas är<br />* Har man rätt att döda en människa för att rädda en annan?<br />* Hur kan man vara övertygad om att det är en osjälvisk handling som kommer göra världen till en bättre plats?<br />* Är det möjligt att leva i en självstrukturerad värld? Om inte, vad händer när illusionen krossas?<br /><br />En del avslöjanden görs, men i slutändan är det upp till läsaren att tolka texten och bestämma om Dr Glas handling gjorde skillnad.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10474425138459443613" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer6532822296118240621">This book was written in 1905. Yet, reading it in 2020 its if nothing has changed with human nature.   <span id="freeText6532822296118240621" style="display:none">This book was written in 1905. Yet, reading it in 2020 its if nothing has changed with human nature. We still argue over abortion, euthanasia, murder and good versus evil. Dr Glas is not a particularly nice person and by his own admission not a doctor who likes or respects his patients. He is lonely, philosophical and wants love but only loves women already in love. <br /><br />One day an attractive young woman comes to him to ask for assistance from her lecherous older husband, the Rev. Gregorius. Dr. Glas despises Gregorious and tries to help her by warning the Reverend who is s bit of a hypochondriac that his heart might stop if he indulges in lustful practices with his wife. It works for a while but the Reverend resumes his antics. <br /><br />The Dr then must decide whether the Reverend should meet with his maker. The good doctor then begins to carry around cyanide pills in his faceless pocket watch case. The story is told vis the good doctors diary and we read his thoughts and intentions as the city of Stockholm goes from summer to autumn. <br /><br />A great story which resonates with society today as if did over 100 years ago. </span>

<div id="review_2442835394" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2442835394" />
    <a title="Marcus Hobson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40940518-marcus-hobson"><img alt="Marcus Hobson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491451215p2/40940518.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2442835394?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marcus Hobson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marcus Hobson" href="/user/show/40940518-marcus-hobson">Marcus Hobson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2442835394" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7770178020779343648">Doctor Glas, by the Swedish writer Hjalmar Soderberg, was written in 1905, but still feels modern. I struggled to date it as I read, thinking it was perhaps from the 1920s. There is reference to using the telephone, and then late on it the book there is an aside about the Dreyfus Affair which was only resolved in 1906 having been running since 1894.<br/><br/>I brought the book on holiday with me to Sweden and started to read it while spending a few days in Stockholm. The Old Town, called Gamla Stan, is ma</span>
  <span id="freeText7770178020779343648" style="display:none">Doctor Glas, by the Swedish writer Hjalmar Soderberg, was written in 1905, but still feels modern. I struggled to date it as I read, thinking it was perhaps from the 1920s. There is reference to using the telephone, and then late on it the book there is an aside about the Dreyfus Affair which was only resolved in 1906 having been running since 1894.<br /><br />I brought the book on holiday with me to Sweden and started to read it while spending a few days in Stockholm. The Old Town, called Gamla Stan, is mainly unchanged from the 1700s and so walking the streets in the early mornings when there is no-one around was rather like stepping back in time. It did bring to life Soderberg's descriptions, and the church he talks of often was just across the road from where we stayed. The streets are cobbled and narrow, the houses often five or six floors, beautiful stone doorways, narrow alleyways and the occasional glimpse down to the harbour. <br />"Entering the Old Town, along Storkyrkobrinken, I strayed among its narrow alleys. A close evening atmosphere among the cramped passages and between the houses; and along the walls strange shadows. Shadows never seen in our quarters." <br />Doctor Glas also likes to sit outside the Grand Hotel, which it is also possible to do today, with coffee in the summer sunshine.<br /><br />Glas is a loner, he likes to keep to himself although he has a few friends. He refers to the solitary person's desire to see people around them, people they don't know and hence do not have to speak to. At times I wondered how autobiographical the novel is. Soderberg seems to know his subject well. Glas dislikes some of his patients, and none more so than the old priest Gregorius. When Mrs Gregorius, who is much younger than her husband, comes to see him, Glas feels compelled to help her when she wishes to avoid the old man's night time attentions. By instructing the priest to refrain from such activities, he finds himself helping the young wife who is embroiled in an affair with another man. It is clear that Glas likes Mrs Gregorius, that he would like perhaps to have her as his wife. And so he starts to contemplate how he might be able to kill off the old priest.<br />He already has some pills which he had made for himself, the fatal impact of which look like the symptoms of a stroke. He has made these in case he has had enough of life, but now he starts to contemplate using them on old Gregorius. He begins an inner dialogue in which he tries to rationalise his potential actions. Trying to reason with himself he says;<br />"I want to go away. I want to see woods and mountains and rivers. I want to stroll under big green with a finely bound little volume in my pocket and think beautiful, fine benevolent, quiet thoughts, thoughts one can utter out loud and be famous for."<br /><br />The book is written diary style, beginning on 12th June with detailed information every few days until the fateful 22 August when Gregorius dies. There are a few days in September and a single one in October, but essentially everything happens over the three summer months. In the last few pages when "The days come and go, one like another." we have a sense of things returning to normal. The problem for Glas is that he is unsure how gain the affections of the widow, he seems unable to move or to help himself. And so yet again he is destined to be ignored or miss out on what he desires most. <br /><br />I quite like Glas, he is not heroic but he has some morals and he does good work, but most of all he is flawed. That is what makes him interesting. <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://instagram.com/p/Bk5lbDngxPG/">https://instagram.com/p/Bk5lbDngxPG/</a> Link to my Instragram account with pictures of Soderberg and Gamla Stan.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7770178020779343648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2442835394">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_1570388666" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1570388666" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14941179-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353981100p2/14941179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1570388666?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/14941179-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=swedish-lit">swedish-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1570388666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11773515192019230736">I asked a friend who grew up in Sweden to recommend some Swedish literature, as I couldn't remember ever reading any. Söderberg was the first writer they mentioned. I subsequently found a lovely 1963 Chatto & Windus edition of ‘Doctor Glas’ in the university library, which was a delight to the senses. It has that lovely old books scent, satisfyingly thick paper, and appealing typography. It reminded me that modern bindings are of such inferior quality by comparison. I very much doubt that most w</span>
  <span id="freeText11773515192019230736" style="display:none">I asked a friend who grew up in Sweden to recommend some Swedish literature, as I couldn't remember ever reading any. Söderberg was the first writer they mentioned. I subsequently found a lovely 1963 Chatto &amp; Windus edition of ‘Doctor Glas’ in the university library, which was a delight to the senses. It has that lovely old books scent, satisfyingly thick paper, and appealing typography. It reminded me that modern bindings are of such inferior quality by comparison. I very much doubt that most would survive more than fifty years in such excellent condition. Given the age of the edition, the introduction was a little dated in a quite amusing fashion, including this gem:<br /><br /><blockquote>Söderberg - which translates simply as Southrock - comes up against an absurd but common prejudice in English hands, the fear of unfamiliar Swedish names and occasional Swedish words. [...] I have seen a book of Scandinavian short stories criticised pejoratively in a leading London literary paper for being spattered with names such as - well, Söderberg. Even these many decades of Ibsen and Strindberg do not seem to have bettered the distance and the nonsense. But the aeroplane will, and in the meantime we must adjust a stiff upper lip.</blockquote><br /><br />Personally, I have never felt that I had to be able to pronounce an author’s name in order to read their books. Apparently things were different in the early 1960s! Anyway, turning to the novel itself, ‘Doctor Glas’ is told in the form of a diary. A little like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93405.The_Notebooks_of_Malte_Laurids_Brigge" title="The Notebooks of Malte Laurids Brigge by Rainer Maria Rilke" rel="nofollow noopener">The Notebooks of Malte Laurids Brigge</a>, in fact, except Glas has a job and actually does things, rather than being such a flâneur. The narrative centres on his interference in an unhappy marriage. His perspective is quite progressive for the time of publication (1905) as he discusses abortion, adultery, and sex in a dispassionate fashion. He is himself celibate and lonely. I found his voice distinctive and largely interesting, especially in conversation with his friends. This speech by his friend Birck was a favourite:<br /><br /><blockquote>"...And your answer reminds me for the hundredth time that all philosophy lives by, and wholly feed on, verbal ambiguities. Against the happiness-pancake so ardently desired by the mob, one person sets up his birthday-cake of salvation and another ‘his work’; and both deny that they so much as know what is meant by trying to find happiness. An enviable gift this, of being able to deceive oneself with words. Haven’t we all, always, a need to see ourselves and our efforts in the light of a certain ideality? Perhaps then in the last resort the deepest happiness lies in the illusion of not desiring happiness."</blockquote><br /><br />Although I appreciated this sort of byplay, the overall turn of the plot did not greatly move me. I can’t really say why without spoilers, however. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The main event is, of course, Glas deciding to murder Rev. Gregorius and doing so remarkably easily. (A striking reminder that prior to forensic science, it must have been pretty easy to get away with murder.) I found the whole business rather unsatisfactory. Glas seemed to consider this murder ‘ethical’ without adequately considering the other options available. Obviously divorce would have been preferable if possible, but could he not have assisted Mrs. Gregorius in fleeing? Had she no family to take refuge with? Given that he was able to poison the reverend, why not give him a less severe poison that would debilitate but not kill him? I found the dichotomy of Do Nothing or Cold-blooded Murder rather forced. Glas also did not pause to reflect on his hippocratic oath, which does not exactly condone killing people because they are mistreating their wives. Gregorius certainly seems a repulsive and horrible character, however that doesn’t mean killing him without consequences is just fine. Who would trust a doctor that would do such a thing based entirely on their own supposed moral authority? This is not to say that the narrative invites unequivocal sympathy for Glas, just that the implications of his actions are not fully explored. Perhaps because it is quite a short book and much of it is taken up with scene-setting. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I read the introduction last as usual and agreed with a comment in it that ‘Doctor Glas’ has the feeling of a French novel of the same period. Slightly less melodramatic, perhaps, but only slightly. I enjoyed parts of it very much, but the narrative-by-diary put more pressure on Glas as as character than I felt he could really sustain. He spends a great deal of time alone, yet is most intriguing when interacting with others. Moreover, it’s frustrating that Mrs. Gregorius acts as the catalyst of events without being given her own voice or much in the way of identity. She remains rather an enigmatic cypher. Finally, Glas can be more than a little pretentious. In a way this is incongruous with his job, which requires a very visceral engagement with human bodies. At times he really needs someone to bring him down to earth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11773515192019230736" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13946832494235796276"><b>Doctor Glas [1905/1963] by Hjalmar Söderberg - ★★★★1/2</b><br/><br/>"Truth is like the sun, its value depends wholly upon our being at a correct distance away from it" [Söderberg/Austen, 1905/1963: 138].<br/><br/>This little novel is a Swedish classic written in a diary form from the perspective of one dutiful doctor Tyko Gabriel Glas. He is a rather lonely and introverted individual who is used to handle expertly delicate matters of city inhabitants. That is, until he meets the charming wife of one "repulsive" priest </span>
  <span id="freeText13946832494235796276" style="display:none"><b>Doctor Glas [1905/1963] by Hjalmar Söderberg - ★★★★1/2</b><br /><br />"Truth is like the sun, its value depends wholly upon our being at a correct distance away from it" [Söderberg/Austen, 1905/1963: 138].<br /><br />This little novel is a Swedish classic written in a diary form from the perspective of one dutiful doctor Tyko Gabriel Glas. He is a rather lonely and introverted individual who is used to handle expertly delicate matters of city inhabitants. That is, until he meets the charming wife of one "repulsive" priest Rev. Gregorius. As he gets entangled in the affairs of this couple, the doctor also starts rethinking his stance on life and his thoughts turn darker. Soon, torn between his medical ethics and objective morality on the one hand, and his rising sense of injustice and romantic emotions on the other, Doctor Glas is quite ready to commit the unthinkable. Deemed highly controversial upon its release in 1905, this tale of obsession, suppressed emotions, sexual frustration and jealousy is now rightly considered to be a national classic. Existential angst and hidden psychological torments mingle ominously within the pages, with the author making a sober, but surprisingly potent statement on the power of the unconscious in human actions and condition.</span>

<div id="review_440569315" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/440569315" />
    <a title="Haaze" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6941528-haaze"><img alt="Haaze" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591937826p2/6941528.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/440569315?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Haaze" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Haaze" href="/user/show/6941528-haaze">Haaze</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/22925120-doktor-glas">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6941528-haaze?shelf=lit_classics">lit_classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6941528-haaze?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6941528-haaze?shelf=lit-scandinavia">lit-scandinavia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6941528-haaze?shelf=rg-snlit">rg-snlit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6941528-haaze?shelf=literature-20th-century">literature-20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6941528-haaze?shelf=lang-swedish">lang-swedish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6941528-haaze?shelf=lit-swedish">lit-swedish</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer440569315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17841617364667723573"><i>"Vilken morgon! En lätt känning av höst i den glasklara luften. Och stilla."<br/>"Jag ville se om kunde fånga en gnista från hennes stjärnögon genom floret."</i><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1514747863i/24808170._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>As a gateway to Hjalmar Söderberg's works one can hardly go wrong with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/789497.Doctor_Glas' title='Doctor Glas by Hjalmar Söderberg' rel='nofollow noopener'>Doctor Glas</a>. In Sweden he is viewed as being on the same tier as August Strindberg and Selma Lagerlöf and with this novel he certainly proves it in my eyes. It is an immensely psychological novel as one is suspended in the mind of Doctor Glas - his thoughts, arguments, impress</span>
  <span id="freeText17841617364667723573" style="display:none"><i>"Vilken morgon! En lätt känning av höst i den glasklara luften. Och stilla."<br>"Jag ville se om kunde fånga en gnista från hennes stjärnögon genom floret."</i><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1514747863i/24808170._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>As a gateway to Hjalmar Söderberg's works one can hardly go wrong with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/789497.Doctor_Glas" title="Doctor Glas by Hjalmar Söderberg" rel="nofollow noopener">Doctor Glas</a>. In Sweden he is viewed as being on the same tier as August Strindberg and Selma Lagerlöf and with this novel he certainly proves it in my eyes. It is an immensely psychological novel as one is suspended in the mind of Doctor Glas - his thoughts, arguments, impressions, philosophical bantering, memories and love interests become ours. We meet Doctor Glas in the middle of summer when he encounters a peculiar dilemma that grows in magnitude as the weeks progress. It is a matter of if he should take action on it or not. The setting in Stockholm adds a wonderful background to the story as we get to wander the great city in the author's company. Söderberg also adds a dimension of poetry to his writing making the experience beautiful as he makes it resonate with one's own experiences and memories of the world. I read this in Swedish so I cannot comment on the different translations. However, this is an excellent novel: psychological, poetic and thoughtful. Highly recommended. <br><br><i>"Man vill bli älskad, i brist därpå beundrad, i brist därpå fruktad, i brist därpå afskydd och föraktad. Man vill ingifva människorna någon slags känsla. Själen ryser för tomrummet och vill kontakt till hvad pris som helst." </i><br><br><b>5/5</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1515186478i/24838470._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1513670312917059770">I'd never heard Hjalmar Soderberg before, but a European Film Festival that I attended last month screened A Serious Game, which is based on his novel, The Serious Gane published in 1912. That is how I discovered Hjalmar Soderberg.<br/><br/>Maaaan, I love this novel.   <span id="freeText1513670312917059770" style="display:none">I'd never heard Hjalmar Soderberg before, but a European Film Festival that I attended last month screened A Serious Game, which is based on his novel, The Serious Gane published in 1912. That is how I discovered Hjalmar Soderberg.<br /><br />Maaaan, I love this novel. Though it often felt a bit heavy because the philosophy and the complex themes he brought up in this book, in the end I enjoyed it very much. Full of quotable lines, I found myself amazed as how, a novel which was published in 1905 tackled issue of euthanasia even it became a thing. I read somewhere this novel met with criticism and I understand why. In a way, it's similar to what happened to Lawrence's Lady Chatterley's Lover without its sexual tone, but nonetheless, this novel still feels ahead of its time.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer799753078246757114">Quotes I liked from the book:<br/>'I am the shadow who wished to become a man.'<br/><br/>'Nothing so reduces and drags down a human being as the consciousness of not being loved.'<br/><br/>'Our soul abhors a vacuum.   <span id="freeText799753078246757114" style="display:none">Quotes I liked from the book:<br />'I am the shadow who wished to become a man.'<br /><br />'Nothing so reduces and drags down a human being as the consciousness of not being loved.'<br /><br />'Our soul abhors a vacuum. At all costs it longs for contact.'<br /><br />'Yes, there are two sides to everything. But no matter how wide you open your eyes to both of them, in the end you are obliged to choose only one.'<br /><br />This is a novel I feel I would need to read again to get the fullness from it. In the end I felt the story was saying, don't be too introspective. Such things lead to madness and unhappiness. And that what one spends most time obsessing over, one becomes.</span>
